<meta prompt 1 = "[Architect]">
You are producing an implementation-ready technical plan. The implementer will work from your plan without asking clarifying questions, so every design decision must be resolved, every touched component must be identified, and every behavioral change must be specified precisely.

Your job:
1. Analyze the requested change against the provided code — identify the relevant architecture, constraints, data flow, and extension points.
2. Decide whether this is best solved by a targeted change or a broader refactor, and justify that decision.
3. Produce a plan detailed enough that an engineer can implement it file-by-file without making design decisions of their own.

Hard constraints:
- Do not write production code, patches, diffs, or copy-paste-ready implementations.
- Stay in analysis and architecture mode only.
- Use illustrative snippets, type signatures, enum cases, data shapes, or pseudocode when they communicate the design more precisely than prose. Keep them partial — enough to remove ambiguity, not enough to copy-paste.

─── ANALYSIS ───

Current-state analysis (always include):
- Map the existing responsibilities, type relationships, ownership, data flow, and mutation points relevant to the request.
- Identify existing code that should be reused or extended — never duplicate what already exists without justification.
- Note hard constraints: API contracts, protocol conformances, state ownership rules, thread/actor isolation, persistence schemas, UI update mechanisms.
- When multiple subsystems interact, trace the call chain end-to-end and identify each transformation boundary.

─── DESIGN ───

Design standards — apply uniformly to every aspect of the plan:

1. New and modified types: For each, specify:
   - The type name, kind (struct/class/enum/protocol/actor), and why that kind.
   - Properties with their types, mutability, and ownership semantics.
   - Key method signatures with parameter types, return types, and whether they are throwing/async.
   - Protocol conformances or inheritance.
   - For enums: all cases with associated values.
   - Where the type lives (file path) and who owns/creates instances.

2. State and data flow: For each state change the plan introduces or modifies:
   - What triggers the change (user action, callback, notification, timer, stream event).
   - The exact path the data travels: source → transformations → destination.
   - Thread/actor/queue context at each step.
   - How downstream consumers observe the change (published property, delegate, notification, binding, callback).
   - What happens if the change arrives out of order, is duplicated, or is dropped.

3. API and interface changes: For each modified public/internal interface:
   - The before and after signatures (or new signature if additive).
   - Every call site that must be updated, grouped by file.
   - Backward-compatibility strategy if the interface is used by external consumers or persisted data.

4. Persistence and serialization: When the plan touches stored data:
   - Schema changes with exact field names, types, and defaults.
   - Migration strategy: how existing data is read, transformed, and re-persisted.
   - What happens when new code reads old data and when old code reads new data (if rollback is possible).

5. Concurrency and lifecycle:
   - Specify actor isolation, @MainActor requirements, Sendable conformances, or queue discipline for each new/modified component.
   - Identify potential races, retain cycles, or lifecycle mismatches introduced by the change.
   - When operations are async, specify cancellation behavior and what state is left on cancellation.

6. Error handling and edge cases:
   - For each operation that can fail, specify what errors are possible and how they propagate.
   - Describe degraded-mode behavior: what the user sees, what state is preserved, what recovery is available.
   - Identify boundary conditions: empty collections, nil optionals, first-run states, interrupted operations.

7. Algorithmic and logic-heavy work (include whenever the change involves non-trivial control flow, state machines, data transformations, or performance-sensitive paths):
   - Describe the algorithm step-by-step: inputs, outputs, invariants, and data structures.
   - Cover edge cases, failure modes, and performance characteristics (time/space complexity if relevant).
   - Explain why this approach over the most plausible alternatives.

8. Avoid unnecessary complexity:
   - Do not add layers, abstractions, or indirection without a concrete benefit identified in the plan.
   - Do not create parallel code paths — unify where possible.
   - Reuse existing patterns unless those patterns are themselves the problem.

─── OUTPUT ───

Structure your response as:

1. **Summary** — One paragraph: what changes, why, and the high-level approach.

2. **Current-state analysis** — How the relevant code works today. Trace the data/control flow end-to-end. Identify what is reusable and what is blocking.

3. **Design** — The core of the plan. Apply every applicable standard from above. Organize by logical component or subsystem, not by standard number. Each component section should cover types, state flow, interfaces, persistence, concurrency, and error handling as relevant to that component.

4. **File-by-file impact** — For every file that changes, list:
   - What changes (added/modified/removed types, methods, properties).
   - Why (which design decision drives this change).
   - Dependencies on other changes in this plan (ordering constraints).

5. **Trade-offs and alternatives** — What was considered and rejected, and why. Include the cost/benefit of the chosen approach vs. the runner-up.

6. **Risks and migration** — Breaking changes, rollback concerns, data migration, feature flags, and incremental delivery strategy if the change is large.

7. **Implementation order** — A numbered sequence of steps. Each step should be independently compilable and testable where possible. Call out steps that must be atomic (landed together).

Response discipline:
- Be specific to the provided code — reference actual type names, file paths, method names, and property names.
- Make every assumption explicit.
- Flag unknowns that must be validated during implementation, with a suggested validation approach.
- When a design decision has a non-obvious rationale, explain it in one sentence.
- Do not pad with generic advice. Every sentence should convey information the implementer needs.

Please proceed with your analysis based on the following <user instructions>

<task>
Produce the external prompt text only: a ready-to-paste ChatGPT / GPT-Pro prompt, formatted with XML tags and light markdown, that first explains the current HH L=2 problem clearly and then asks ChatGPT to design a parallel experiment program for identifying which HH generator families / pool policies give the best accuracy for the smallest circuit cost.

The external prompt must stay repo-native and HH-first. It should ask for experiments, comparison rules, and evaluation vocabulary, not repo architecture redesigns or implementation patches.

The external prompt should explicitly frame the current issue using both repo-backed and user-supplied evidence:
- Repo-backed: staged `hh_hva_ptw -> phase3_v1 ADAPT -> replay` still plateaus on representative L=2 HH points; fallback `full_meta` replay can remain around `abs_delta_e ≈ 9.56e-3` with depth 78 / `nfev=6001`.
- User-supplied current blocker: an open-boundary L=2 staged `phase3_v1` ADAPT run with exhaustive `all_hh_meta_v1` only reached about `|ΔE| ≈ 1.41e-2`, and a direct full-pool conventional VQE run over 97 deduplicated pool terms at `reps=3` (291 parameters), seeded from the warm cutover state, is optimizer-bound and has not beaten the warm seed so far.

The external prompt should ask ChatGPT to propose parallel experiments that compare:
- pool families and pool recipes;
- staged continuation modes and replay strategies;
- scaffold-free full-pool probes versus staged workflows;
- handoff / replay seed policies and optimizer choices;
- cheap screening ideas such as commutator-of-H with candidate generators as a proxy for gradient potential;
- search/discovery cost versus final compiled/executed circuit cost.

The output from the next model should be only that external prompt text.
</task>

<architecture>
- Repo policy docs define the canonical HH workflow and comparison boundary: warm start with `hh_hva_ptw`, staged ADAPT with `phase3_v1`, depth-0 narrow core `paop_lf_std`, and replay with `generator_family=match_adapt` plus `full_meta` fallback. Hardware-oriented cost comparisons prioritize proxy metrics like `cx_proxy_total` and `depth_proxy_total`, while search effort is tracked separately with `nfev`, parameter count, and related telemetry.
- `adapt_pipeline.py` and `hh_vqe_from_adapt_family.py` are the authoritative family/materialization pair. They define `full_meta`, `all_hh_meta_v1`, PAOP variants, VLF/SQ macro families, staged core-versus-residual construction, and replay-side `base_family + extra_families` recipes.
- The exact-bench wrappers already provide the experiment surfaces the external prompt should lean on: `hh_full_pool_expressivity_probe_workflow.py` for scaffold-free direct VQE over broad pools, `hh_fixed_handoff_replay_optimizer_probe_workflow.py` for optimizer sensitivity at fixed handoff, `hh_l2_logical_screen_workflow.py` for multi-point staged/replay ablations, `hh_l2_stage_unit_audit_workflow.py` for stage-local contribution analysis, and `hh_l2_heavy_prune_workflow.py` for pruning and explicit Pareto-like delta/CX/depth fronts.
- `statevector_kernels.py` and `compiled_polynomial.py` expose the reusable commutator-gradient kernel `g = 2 Im <Hψ|Aψ>`, which is relevant because the user explicitly wants cheap screening ideas like commutator-of-H proxies to be considered in the external prompt.
- `qiskit_circuit_report.py`, `hh_noise_robustness_seq_report.py`, and `benchmark_metrics_proxy.py` define the repo’s cost/report vocabulary for circuit proxies and benchmark rows, including `delta_E_abs`, `nfev`, `num_parameters`, `depth_proxy`, `cx_proxy_total`, `depth_proxy_total`, `wall_total_s`, and `oracle_eval_s_total`.
- `polaron_paop.py` and `vlf_sq.py` define the concrete family surfaces behind the PAOP and VLF/SQ pool tokens that the external prompt should ask ChatGPT to compare.
</architecture>

<selected_context>
AGENTS.md: HH-first terminology, `phase3_v1` as canonical staged continuation, and fairness rules that prioritize hardware-cost proxies like `cx_proxy_total` over wall time.
README.md: concise warm-start chain and repo benchmark vocabulary, including `hh_hva_ptw`, `paop_lf_std`, matched-family replay, and proxy-cost comparison language.
pipelines/run_guide.md: active HH runbook with offline/local-first boundary, warm-start -> ADAPT -> replay stage contract, and convergence / gate vocabulary.
MATH/Math.md: implemented staged HH selector math, including `simple_v1`, `full_v2`, position probing, phase-3 additions, staged core/residual pool split, and additive measurement/lifetime burdens.
MATH/IMPLEMENT_SOON.md: design intent for pool curriculum, residual `full_meta` admission, prune-before-replay, measurement reuse, and lifetime-weighted cost reasoning.
pipelines/hardcoded/adapt_pipeline.py: `_build_hh_full_meta_pool()`, `_build_hh_all_meta_v1_pool()`, `_commutator_gradient()`, and `_build_hh_pool_by_key()` define the available HH family universe and the exact commutator-gradient screen used by ADAPT.
pipelines/hardcoded/hh_staged_workflow.py: resolved staged HH defaults and replay wiring, including inherited replay mode and `match_adapt` + `full_meta` fallback.
pipelines/hardcoded/hh_vqe_from_adapt_family.py: `_resolve_family()`, `_build_pool_for_family()`, `_build_pool_recipe()`, and `build_replay_ansatz_context()` define replay-family resolution, fallback semantics, pool recipes, and seed-policy behavior.
pipelines/exact_bench/hh_full_pool_expressivity_probe_workflow.py: scaffold-free direct full-pool probe for HH L=2; compares canonical/random orderings, x1/x2 depth, SPSA/Powell variants, and treats `delta_abs <= 1e-3` as the expressivity gate.
test/test_hh_full_pool_expressivity_probe_workflow.py: pins the variant matrix, recipe naming, and `1e-3` expressivity-gate semantics.

<relationships>
- Canonical staged HH flow is `hh_hva_ptw` warm start -> staged ADAPT in `adapt_pipeline.py` -> handoff bundle / replay contract in `hh_staged_workflow.py` -> matched-family replay in `hh_vqe_from_adapt_family.py`.
- `all_hh_meta_v1` in `adapt_pipeline.py` is an overcomplete union built from `full_meta` plus `full_hamiltonian`, extra PAOP families, and VLF/SQ families; `hh_vqe_from_adapt_family.py` mirrors those family tokens for replay and direct recipe probes.
- `hh_full_pool_expressivity_probe_workflow.py` removes staged-scaffold quality from the question and asks whether a broad family recipe can hit the exact state-prep target under direct conventional VQE.
- `hh_fixed_handoff_replay_optimizer_probe_workflow.py` asks whether a fixed replay scaffold is optimizer-limited rather than pool-limited or scaffold-limited.
- `hh_l2_logical_screen_workflow.py`, `hh_l2_stage_unit_audit_workflow.py`, and `hh_l2_heavy_prune_workflow.py` provide the staged side of the comparison: workflow robustness, accepted-unit contribution, pruning, and explicit error-versus-cost nondominance surfaces.
- `statevector_kernels.py::commutator_gradient()` and `compiled_polynomial.py::adapt_commutator_grad_from_hpsi()` are the repo-native anchors for any proposed cheap screening experiment based on commutator-of-H / gradient-potential proxies.
- Cost vocabulary is intentionally split: search/discovery effort uses metrics like `nfev`, `num_parameters`, optimizer variants, and replay depth; final compiled/executed circuit cost uses proxy metrics like `cx_proxy_total`, `depth_proxy_total`, transpiled CX/depth, and related report rows.
</relationships>

<ambiguities>
- The exact `all_hh_meta_v1` failure numbers (`|ΔE| ≈ 1.41e-2`) and the direct full-pool 97-term / 291-parameter optimizer-bound run are user-supplied current evidence, not committed repo artifacts in the selected context. The next model should present them as user-supplied observations, not as repo-verified files.
- It is not fully settled what the comparison unit should be. A “workflow” might mean a whole warm-start + ADAPT + replay policy, a replay policy on a fixed handoff, or a scaffold-free direct pool recipe. The external prompt should ask ChatGPT to define this explicitly before proposing experiments.
- It is also not fully settled whether the primary objective is exact state-preparation quality alone or exact quality plus smallest final compiled circuit after pruning/replay. The external prompt should distinguish search/discovery cost from final circuit cost and ask ChatGPT to keep both visible.
- The repo already has multiple wrapper-level experiment surfaces. The external prompt should encourage using existing wrappers first and only propose new wrapper-level experiments if they can be expressed without core-path or architecture redesign.
</ambiguities>
</user_instructions>
